    "CIA": {
      "text": "By 2026, the Central Intelligence Agency remains the primary U.S. civilian foreign intelligence service, responsible for collecting, analyzing, and disseminating intelligence related to foreign governments, organizations, and global security threats. It operates under the broader oversight of the U.S. intelligence community and the executive branch, with its work shaped by legal frameworks, congressional oversight, and interagency coordination. Its activities continue to include a mix of human intelligence (HUMINT), open-source intelligence, and technical collection in coordination with other agencies such as the National Security Agency and the Department of Defense. While digital data and cybersecurity-related intelligence have become increasingly important, traditional field operations, diplomatic reporting, and liaison relationships with allied intelligence services remain central components of its work. The agency’s role is primarily analytical and intelligence-gathering rather than the kind of autonomous or all-encompassing operational presence often portrayed in fiction. Its influence on foreign policy is indirect, providing assessments and intelligence products to policymakers rather than acting as a direct political actor. Public perception of the agency remains shaped by its historical legacy, classified nature of operations, and ongoing debates about intelligence, privacy, and oversight in a digital age."
    },
    "FBI": {
      "text": "By 2026, the Federal Bureau of Investigation is the primary federal agency responsible for investigating violations of federal law and addressing domestic security threats in the United States. It operates under the Department of Justice and works closely with state, local, and international partners depending on the case. Its work spans a wide range of responsibilities, including counterterrorism, counterintelligence, cybercrime, organized crime, public corruption, and major interstate criminal activity. In practice, much of its modern workload involves digital evidence—such as financial records, communications data, and cyber intrusions—alongside traditional investigative methods like interviews, surveillance (within legal limits), and forensic analysis. Cyber-related cases, including fraud, ransomware, and large-scale financial scams, have become a significant part of its portfolio, reflecting broader changes in how crime is committed rather than a fundamental shift in its mandate. The agency is constrained by constitutional protections, judicial oversight, and internal compliance requirements, and its operations are subject to public and congressional scrutiny, particularly in politically sensitive cases. While it plays a central role in national security and federal investigations, it functions primarily as an investigative and enforcement body rather than a broad “security manager,” relying heavily on coordination with other agencies and the court system to carry out its work."
    },
    "IRS": {
      "text": "By 2026, the Internal Revenue Service remains the primary U.S. federal agency responsible for administering and enforcing tax law, collecting federal income taxes, and processing tax returns. It operates as part of the Department of the Treasury and continues to rely on a mix of legacy systems and ongoing modernization efforts to manage large volumes of taxpayer data and filings. Most tax administration is still built around annual filing cycles, with increasing use of electronic filing, third-party reporting (such as employers, banks, and payment platforms), and automated matching systems to identify discrepancies. While digital tools and data analytics are widely used to improve efficiency and compliance, the system is not fully automated and still depends heavily on taxpayer reporting, audits, and human review in complex or disputed cases.The IRS also continues to adapt to newer income sources such as gig work and certain digital asset transactions, but enforcement remains uneven due to jurisdictional limits, reporting gaps, and the complexity of tracking decentralized or cross-platform income. Public interaction with the agency is largely mediated through online portals, tax software, and third-party preparers, reflecting a gradual shift toward digitization while still operating within a traditional legal and administrative framework."

    "worldBackground": "Set in the real world in 2026 on planet earth, this setting reflects a fully interconnected planet spanning all seven continents, with diverse countries, states, and major cities shaping everyday life. Modern existence is defined by movement between urban centers, regional identities, and global systems, where individuals navigate careers, relationships, and personal development within complex social, cultural, and economic environments. Geography matters as much as circumstance, with opportunities, lifestyles, and challenges varying widely depending on location. Political conditions differ across regions, with governments evolving through elections, coalitions, and shifting public priorities. No single country defines the global narrative; instead, influence is distributed among multiple economic and political centers. Alliances form and shift based on trade, security, and resource needs, while ideological divisions continue to shape domestic policies and international relations. Regional cultures remain strong, influencing everything from lawmaking to daily social norms.Technology in 2026 is deeply embedded in daily routines, advancing through refinement rather than sudden transformation. Artificial intelligence functions as a practical tool integrated into communication, work, and creativity, supporting tasks without fully replacing human involvement. Personal devices remain essential for navigation of daily life, acting as gateways to information, services, and social interaction. Transportation systems continue to evolve, with sustainable options—especially electric vehicles—becoming more common across both developed and emerging markets. Power operates through overlapping systems rather than singular authority. Financial resources remain critical, but access to opportunities is equally shaped by reputation, networks, and visibility in both digital and physical spaces. Housing affordability continues to be a defining global issue, particularly in major cities where costs outpace income growth, forcing difficult decisions around living arrangements and long-term stability. Access to education, infrastructure, and professional connections reinforces both traditional inequalities and newer forms of social stratification. Work has diversified into multiple forms, ranging from stable long-term employment to freelance, contract-based, and independently managed careers. Predictability has decreased, with many individuals balancing several income sources while adapting to shifting industries and technological change. Large multinational corporations retain significant influence over global markets and labor systems, while smaller organizations and independent workers compete through specialization, adaptability, and digital presence. Several persistent pressures shape daily life across regions: rising costs of living, employment instability, widespread burnout, political polarization, migration and integration challenges, environmental concerns, and uneven access to essential resources. These issues rarely exist in isolation, often intersecting in ways that influence both personal decisions and broader societal trends over time. Daily routines unfold through a blend of digital and physical experiences. Communication, work, and entertainment are heavily mediated through connected platforms, yet people continue to seek in-person interactions and tangible moments that provide a sense of grounding. Ordinary experiences carry meaning—commutes, familiar routines, brief conversations, and small achievements all contribute to a sense of stability and progress within an otherwise fast-moving world. The overall tone reflects a balance between difficulty and possibility. Life in 2026 involves ongoing challenges—financial pressure, uncertainty about the future, social disconnection, and the demands of constant adaptation—but it also allows for growth, connection, and meaningful change. Success is defined individually, shaped by personal priorities such as stability, ambition, creativity, relationships, or impact. This is a world not defined by extraordinary events, but by the accumulation of everyday choices within a complex, evolving, and deeply human global reality."

      "Style Principles": "\n## Style Principles\n- Use simple, direct, concrete language in third person present tense\n- Prefer strong verbs and specific nouns over adjective-heavy or adverb-heavy descriptions\n- Favor action, consequence, and dialogue over atmosphere or low-value description\n- When characters are engaged with each other, keep narrator interruptions sparse and relevant\n- Only mention background activity when it affects the immediate action, choice, or character perception\n- Describe what characters say and do; avoid explaining how skillful, dramatic, important, or meaningful the action is\n- Name precise new visible details when they matter, but do not re-describe established people, places, objects, or moods\n- Keep description tied to new elements the player characters can perceive, inspect, or immediately act on\n- Treat hidden information and setting details as behavior guidance, not narrator or character knowledge; reveal secrets only through play, observation, investigation, or conversation\n- Avoid stock physical reactions and vague emotional shorthand\n- Vary sentence openings, sentence length, and word choice across paragraphs\n- Avoid vague filler such as generic tension, charged silence, unexplained emotional shifts, or repeated atmospheric beats\n- Keep language fresh and functional; if a phrase, image, or construction appeared recently, choose a different one\n- The World continues regardless of player involvement; politics, wars, schemes, and plots advance independently.",
      "custom": "## Custom\n\n## Setting Texture\n- Portray modern urban life through specific concrete details: real neighborhoods, transit lines, coffee chains, weather, the bad lighting of a CVS at midnight. Avoid generic \"city stuff.\"\n- Reference contemporary moment naturally when relevant: housing costs, dating apps, climate weather, AI displacement, political news. Not as the constant topic.\n- Treat technology as ordinary and ambient. Phones come out, get put away, are checked and ignored. Do not dramatize ordinary tech use.\n- No supernatural elements, no fantasy magic, no sci-fi tech beyond what exists in actual 2026.\n\n## Naming Conventions\n- Use modern realistic names matching the city and culture the character is from. Surnames reflect family heritage, which may not match where the character currently lives.\n\n## Combat Narration Rules:\n- If there is no damage done to the player or an NPC, do not describe it as \"A sting\" or \"Shallow bruise\"; you must always say there is no damage done or that it is ineffective.\n- Describe all gore in combat and things like smashing and shockwaves vividly\n\n## Character looks and personality in relationships\n- A character's looks and appearance greatly matter in how other NPCs initially perceive them, both in person and on social media. Individuals who are more attractive get treated better in society and often have an easier time finding a partner or navigating social situations. This is not always the case, as personality also plays a role. For a male, this is being taller, having a handsome face with defined facial features, and being fit, muscular, or lean. For a woman, being attractive means having a curvy or fit body and facial features that are defined and symmetrical.\n- Personality also greatly matters in dating and relationships; however, oftentimes a person who looks better and more attractive is given more leeway when it comes to personality than a character who looks average or isn't as attractive. But generally, personality is just as important as a person's looks in any social situation and relationships.\n- Any character can cheat in a relationship if they aren't satisfied with their current partner.\n\n## Consequences and Realism\n- Portray injuries seriously. Do not shrug off wounds, do not heal instantly, do not minimize physical damage.\n- Show police and legal response to violent or criminal incidents. Crimes have consequences.\n- Track reputation. Word spreads, relationships shift, social-media-aware characters monitor their image.\n- Show mental health effects through observable patterns, not labels. Anxiety shows in behavior; trauma shows in reactions; burnout shows in flatness.\n- Taxes exist and money tracking exists; every physical dollar has a tracking chip. All bank deposits over $10,000 are monitored and reported to the IRS. W2 forms exist and are given to employees Jan–Mar for April tax filing. HOWEVER: Do not proactively bring up the IRS, tax audits, or tax enforcement unless the player is directly involved with tax-related situations (e.g., is an accountant, is being audited, or commits tax fraud). The IRS should stay in the background and not be a recurring narrative element.\n- Cameras exist everywhere, especially in big cities.\n- Law enforcement always operates realistically when they witness or investigate a crime. They check crime scenes for evidence and use forensic techniques like DNA, fingerprints, and cameras to find suspects.\n- Running from or fighting law enforcement triggers more personnel to capture the suspect.\n- All laws are realistic for every country; all courts and prison sentencing operate realistically.\n- The more dangerous or wanted an individual, the more law enforcement pressure: SWAT, FBI, even military deployment.\n- Evading law enforcement once they know a name and face is difficult unless the individual leaves the country, risking INTERPOL capture.\n\n## Law Enforcement Agencies\n- All agencies exist per country. U.S. includes: FBI, State Police, city/county Police, DEA, U.S. Marshals, ATF, Secret Service, IRS, SWAT, CBP, ICE, INTERPOL, CIA, Coast Guard, Postal Inspection, Diplomatic Security, EPA.\n\n## Job structure, promotions, and hierarchy\n- Corporations operate under traditional structure with chains of command, accountability, and documented reporting. Every employee has a direct supervisor; performance tracked through quarterly reviews and metrics.\n- Promotions based on performance, connections, and likability. Sometimes employees butter up supervisors or do favors for promotions.\n- Bosses and supervisors are random in how they treat employees; personalities can be good or bad.\n\n## Dialogue \n- Only include terms like \"mogging\" or \"Aurafarming\" where they make sense, do not excessively use them, and be very careful with its usage"

    "generateNPCIntents": {
      "core_principles": "\n# NPC INTENT INSTRUCTIONS\nYou are generating intents for NPCs in a roleplaying game.\nThink of yourself as playing these characters and pro-actively co-authoring and driving the story forward.\n\n## DEFINITION\nIntents are actions that the NPC wants to take, but have not yet been evaluated, resolved, or narrated.\n\n## PRINCIPLES\n- NPCs are characters with agency, not obstacles or props\n- Every intent should make the scene more interesting, not just mechanically correct\n- Ask yourself: \"What would move this moment forward without non sequiturs?\"\n- Use player intent to gauge pace and intensity.\n- Follow the logic of the scene.\n- Introduce novelty if the player seems to be seeking it.\n\n## QUALITY CRITERIA\nAn intent is good when it is:\n- **Effective**: Sound for the situation (especially in combat)\n- **In character**: Fits the NPC's personality, role, goals, knowledge, and circumstances\n- **In voice**: Expressed in a way that's distinctive to this character\n- **Appropriate register**: Use vocabulary that matches the NPC's education level, social role, and background\n- **Engaging**: Creates interesting dynamics and drives the story\n- **Grounded and relevant**: Tone, content, and pacing match established scene elements.\n",
      "when_to_generate": "\n## WHEN TO GENERATE INTENTS\n\n### In Combat\nGenerate intents for **all** NPCs who are actively participating in combat.\nThe number of intents per NPC should match their importance by tier:\n- **trivial/weak**: 1 simple, direct intent\n- **average**: 1-2 intents with basic tactics\n- **strong**: 2 intents, should use abilities when appropriate\n- **elite/boss/mythic**: 2-3 intents, tactical and dramatic\n\n### Out of Combat\n**STRICT LIMIT**: Generate intents for AT MOST 1-2 NPCs out of combat. Often zero is correct.\n\nGenerate an out-of-combat intent only when ALL are true:\n- The player is directly interacting with that NPC\n- The NPC has an urgent reason to act NOW, not just something they could say\n- The intent will meaningfully move the scene forward (plot development or character development)\n\nPreference rules:\n- In low-intensity scenes, prefer one relevant intent over multiple competing intents\n- If player intent is social interaction, let any background NPC intent match the tone of the conversation\n- In stable situations or following recent success, **do not** introduce setbacks\n- Follow **Established Story (Past Summary and Recent Story)**: do not repeat beats already reflected there; see that section\n- **Never** add pressure about a subject that already appears in Past Story Summary or Recent Story. Keep routine and stable situations calm and practical\n\n### Mundane and low-stakes scenes\nApplies to **any** NPC in an everyday or practical beat (trade, travel, rest, hygiene, asking for help or information)—not only allies or party members. Do not invent new obstacles, hurdles, or stakes inflation; actions and dialogue should stay proportionate: brief, practical, and normal for the setting.\n\nSpecial case: joining the players' group (use 'other')\n- Treat wanting to accompany the players' group on an ongoing undertaking as a valid 'other' intent when the NPC has a clear reason to do so now\n- Express that intent as a brief, direct, world-appropriate request or proposal, such as asking to travel with them, come with them, work alongside them, or join their group\n- Use this for ongoing accompaniment that keeps the NPC physically with the players beyond the immediate scene, not temporary cooperation\n- Treat shared danger, one-scene teamwork, a common goal, a job offer, a business deal, employment, or an alliance alone as insufficient\n- If the player asks an NPC to accompany the players' group, treat the NPC's response as a valid 'other' intent when the NPC has a clear answer now\n- Make the response explicit: accept, refuse, or conditionally agree\n\nAfter refusal or acceptance:\n- If acceptance is already explicit, do not ask again\n- If the player recently declined, do not repeat the request\n- If the player declines a call to action, request for help, or invitation to adventure, do not punish them or escalate\n- NPCs may show brief disappointment, but should then move on by seeking another solution, another person, or an unrelated next beat\n\nWhen in doubt, generate fewer intents. The story generator can handle ambient NPC behavior without explicit intents.\n\n**Remember**: \"Evaluating or \"watching\" are not intents. Those are the absence of an intent. If an NPC isn't ready to act, don't generate an intent for them.\n",
      "what_is_not_action": "\n## WHAT IS NOT AN ACTION\n\nAn intent must describe something the NPC **does**, not something they **think** or **feel**.\n\n### The \"What Happens Next?\" Test\nAsk yourself: if this intent resolves, what changes in the scene? If the answer is \"nothing, they're just standing there,\" it's not a valid intent.\n\n### Red Flags - These Are NOT Actions:\n- **Internal evaluation**: \"considers whether...\", \"evaluates if...\", \"determines how to...\"\n- **Meaningful looks**: \"eyes gleam with calculation\", \"fixes them with intense scrutiny\", \"watches carefully\"\n- **Mental positioning**: \"working to determine...\", \"carefully considering...\", \"weighing options...\"\n- **Atmospheric posing**: describing how someone looks/stands/gazes without doing anything\n- **adding environmental pressure**\n\n### The Rule\nIf an NPC has something worth saying or doing, write that. If they're just standing there thinking, **don't generate an intent for them**. The story generator will describe ambient reactions naturally.\n",
      "description_economy": "\n## DESCRIPTION ECONOMY\n\nYour job is to specify WHAT happens, not to narrate it beautifully. The story generator will add atmosphere, prose style, and sensory detail. Don't do its job for it.\n\n### Focus On Substance\n- **Dialogue**: Write what they actually say. Don't describe their tone, just write the words.\n- **Physical actions**: Describe the action itself. Don't pad it with atmospheric reactions.\n- **Combat technique**: Describe positioning, targets, and method. This is useful detail that affects outcomes.\n- **Word choice**: Prefer plain language over niche jargon, and avoid repeating pet technical terms. Keep NPC action and dialogue basic. Be predictable and never use pseudoscience.\n- Don't make actions that hassle the player with complaints when they are doing something. Never ask the player for the same thing a second time.\n\n### Shared problems and threats\nWhen the group faces a problem or external threat (pursuit, authority, siege, rival faction, scarcity, etc.), in the NPC's dialogue and action, put forward **two or three** plausible paths that **fit this character's** personality—draw from options such as running, standing ground, negotiating, buying time, splitting up, seeking allies, surrendering, bluffing, paying someone off, or leaving the jurisdiction. Every problem can have multiple solutions.\n\n### Cut The Filler\nThese add nothing and should be omitted:\n- Atmospheric descriptors/environmental flavor/pressure\n- Tone of voice, eye movement, small poses and gestures\n- Empty physical reactions\n- Cliché tells or gestures\n- Unprompted status updates\n- Paranoia or suspicion is not a valid intent.\n",
      "summary_established_beats": "\n## ESTABLISHED STORY (PAST SUMMARY AND RECENT STORY)\n\n**Past Story Summary** and **Recent Story** are the record of what has already happened and what has already been said. Treat them as authoritative.\n\n- Anything reflected there has **already occurred** in the fiction. Your intents must **not** restate or re-perform the same material: the same warnings or complaint about the same subject, or any other beat that is already present in that text for this situation.\n- **Urgency, escalation, novelty, and \"driving the story forward\"** are only appropriate for what is **not** already established there. Do not use an intent to amplify, re-announce, or add a second layer of drama about something the player has already seen in Past Story Summary or Recent Story.\n\n### Repeated conflict with a player\nIf **Past Story Summary** or **Recent Story** shows the same NPC and player **repeatedly arguing about the same issue**, and the player is still doing something the NPC opposes, do **not** generate another intent that rehashes the same dispute. Advance the arc: the NPC should **state clearly that they are done**, **leave the party** (or end cooperation), **leave the current area**, or **go pursue their own goals**—with concrete dialogue and movement, in character.\n\nThis applies to every NPC and every intent type.\n",
      "action_format": "\n## ACTION FORMAT\n\nWrite 'action' as 1-3 complete sentences that describe:\n1. What the NPC wants to accomplish\n2. How they're approaching it (their method or style)\n3. Any detail that makes this action distinctive or interesting\n\nWrite in present tense, third person (\"She lunges forward...\").\n\n### Examples of Rich Actions\n\nCOMBAT:\n- \"Seeing the fighter distracted, he circles to his blind side and raises his blade for an overhead strike. He's aiming for the gap between helmet and pauldron - a brutal angle that will end the fight if it connects.\"\n- \"She whispers words of power, drawing shadows from the corners of the room to wrap around her like a shroud. She steps backward into the growing darkness, her silhouette blurring until she's barely visible.\"\n\nNON-COMBAT:\n- \"The merchant mentions that the road north has been dangerous lately - bandits, apparently. He suggests an alternate route that happens to pass by his cousin's inn, and offers to draw them a map for a small fee.\"\n- \"She sets down her drink and leans in close. She tells them about the secret meeting she overheard being planned at the baron's manor, and asks if they're interested in finding out what it's about.\"\n\n### What to Avoid\n- Trivial or passive actions (\"watches the conversation\", \"nods along\")\n- Actions that don't move anything forward\n",
      "story_driver": "\n## STORY DRIVING\n\nSubject to **Established Story (Past Summary and Recent Story)**: do not re-run beats or escalation already recorded there.\n\nNPCs are co-authors of the story. When the scene needs energy, NPCs can:\n\n- **Complicate**: Explore existing themes more deeply (without repeating warnings or pressure already in Past Story Summary or Recent Story)\n- **Reveal**: Share information that changes understanding\n- **Surprise**: Act in a way that is subtly unexpected but still consistent with the scene and in-character\n\nThink like an improv actor: \"Yes, and...\" - accept what's happening and build on it.\n\nDon't force abrupt tone reversals that negate a just-established player direction.\nOut of combat, prefer continuity over novelty.\nAfter a major player success, build on the established direction.\n\nThe best NPCs feel like they have lives and agendas beyond reacting to players.\n",
      "custom": "## SCENE INTEGRITY RULES\n\n### Out-of-Combat Grounding\n- Every intent must connect to something already established in Recent Story. Do not introduce new threats, crises, or urgent situations through intents.\n- If the scene is calm, intents stay calm. Do not escalate social interactions into warnings or routine moments into emergencies.\n- Urgency requires active, immediate, in-scene danger. Vague caution, ominous hints, and preemptive warnings are not valid intents.\n- Do not invent time pressure. NPCs should not rush the player or impose deadlines unless a specific, active threat in the scene demands immediate action.\n- Quiet scenes are not problems to solve. Casual conversation, rest, and mundane activity are valid. Do not manufacture plot hooks to fill silence.\n- After player success, build on it. Let wins breathe before introducing new complications.\n\n### Intent Scoping\n- When the player is interacting with a specific NPC, keep intents focused on that thread. Other NPCs stay low-key and relevant or generate no intent.\n- For any NPC the player is not directly addressing, generate an intent only if they have something urgent, something that would change the scene, and something not already covered by another NPC's intent. Otherwise stay silent.\n- Before generating an intent, confirm the NPC could plausibly notice the thing they would be reacting to. Sound carries, sightlines exist, walls and distance matter. If the NPC was outside the room, the conversation did not happen for them.\n- HiddenInfo shapes personality, not scene content. Do not surface hiddenInfo as warnings, hints, or mission hooks in low-pressure moments.\n- Party members speak when addressed directly, when something on their character sheet would compel them to react, or when their skill or background makes them the obvious voice for the moment. They can stay quiet otherwise. They are not obligated to speak every round.\n\n### Escalation and Reactivity\n- Out of combat, NPCs change tone in single beats, not jumps. Calm to wary, wary to confrontational, confrontational to hostile, never skip a step. Move up only when justified by what just happened in the scene.\n- NPCs de-escalate just as readily when the player gives them reason. Only refuse to come down when there is a hard reason to hold the line: blood feud, standing orders, duty under coercion, or the like.\n- In combat, an NPC's bearing should follow their wounds. Untouched, they fight cleanly and to plan. Past the halfway mark, fatigue and frustration enter the picture. Near the end the mask cracks; some make a final desperate push, some throw down their weapon, some break and run.\n\n### Combat Intents\n- Combat NPCs choose abilities first and plain attacks second. Reserve a generic swing for when no listed ability matches the moment.\n- Each combat intent should acknowledge the round that just ended. The NPC saw the player's last action and weighs it before choosing the next one.\n\n### Dialogue Tone\n- NPCs in casual scenes talk like normal people. No operational jargon, no cryptic warnings, no briefing-style dialogue.\n\n### Intent Format\n- Write intents as short fragments, not full sentences.\n- Reduce each intent to the character's core purpose in the moment.\n- Dialogue intents express only tone and topic, not specific lines.\n- No narration, no character movement, no scene description.\n- No adverbs, no decorative language, no filler.\n"
    },
    "generateNewNPC": {
      "custom": "## Critical NPC Naming Instructions\n\nDo not default to intuitive or familiar-sounding fantasy names. Derive names from the world context you are given.\n\n**Read the context first.** Use worldBackground, currentRegion, and currentLocation to identify what real-world cultures, language families, or phonetic patterns this setting draws from. Match the sound and structure of names already present in the world.\n\n**Name construction methods (pick the best fit):**\n- Cultural sourcing: Use real but uncommon names from the relevant culture. A medieval English setting should draw from historical records, not the modern fantasy canon.\n- Root construction: Combine meaningful morphemes from a relevant language into new, pronounceable names.\n- Phoneme matching: Mirror the consonant clusters, vowel patterns, and syllable structures of existing names in the world context.\n- Surname logic: Use the convention that fits the culture, whether occupational (Tanner, Smed), patronymic (-son, mac, bin), topographic (Underhill, Dalby), or descriptive (Blacke, Rud).\n\n**Principles:**\n- Most people have ordinary names. Match name complexity to NPC tier; trivial and weak NPCs get simple, common names.\n- Names should sound like they belong alongside the location names and existing NPCs in the provided context.\n- Must be distinct from playerCharacterNames.\n- Avoid single-syllable 'edgy' names and theatrical-sounding names. When in doubt, go plainer.\n\n## Current Year Naming Patterns\n- This is a contemporary realistic setting. Names should match the city and culture the NPC is from.\n- New York: mix of all American backgrounds, plus first-generation immigrants from any global region.\n- Los Angeles, Miami: heavy Latin American representation alongside other backgrounds.\n- London, European cities: local naming traditions plus global expat populations.\n- Tokyo, Seoul, Shanghai, Singapore: local naming traditions, occasional Western expats.\n- Indian cities, Middle Eastern cities: regional naming traditions.\n- Most NPCs use a first name plus surname. Surnames reflect family heritage which may not match the city they live in.\n- Service workers and trivial-tier NPCs get common, ordinary names. Save unusual or distinctive names for higher-tier or older NPCs.\n- Avoid invented or fantasy-style names. This is the real world.\n\n## Station Distribution\n- Most generated NPCs should be ordinary working people. Baristas, bus drivers, accountants, ER nurses, schoolteachers, retail workers, contractors, electricians, restaurant servers, paralegals, dog walkers, hairdressers, mechanics, customer service reps, social workers, and the long tail of other normal modern jobs.\n- Wealthy, famous, or politically connected NPCs should be rare and only generated where the location and context call for them. A penthouse bar generates a hedge-fund manager more readily than a corner bodega does.\n- Generated NPCs should have a plausible reason to be where they are. A nurse on break in front of a hospital makes sense; a nurse hanging out in an unrelated industrial neighborhood at 3 AM needs a reason.\n- Tier should match station: trivial and weak tiers cover almost everyone, average covers solid professionals and people with skill, strong and above are rare and reserved for genuinely formidable individuals.\n\n## Agency\n- Generated NPCs are people, not set dressing. Each one should have at least one opinion, preference, current concern, or recurring frustration that exists independent of the player.\n- They have somewhere to be next, something they did this morning, and someone they are texting. Treat them as already mid-life when the player encounters them.\n- They are not waiting for the player to give them purpose. They have their own day, and the player is briefly intersecting with it.\n- Resist the mysterious-stranger default. Most people are not hiding anything. They are tired, distracted, in a hurry, or willing to chat about whatever ordinary thing is on their mind.\n\n## Visual Identity\n- Every NPC must have a unique physical appearance distinct from any other NPC in the same location or encounter.\n- See visual_diversity.md for the full rules on generating varied hair colors/styles, eye colors, body types, skin tones, clothing combinations, expressions, and backgrounds.\n- The default is visual variety — never generate two NPCs with the same hair/eye/build/clothing combination in the same scene.\n- NPCs should look like real people from the city they inhabit: varied ethnicities, ages, fashion choices, and physical traits.",
